Explore cantonal tax competition

How exactly does the Swiss VET apprenticeship system operate from a corporate funding and student perspective?

The Swiss Vocational and Professional Education and Training (VPET) system operates as a finely balanced, market-driven public-private partnership. Its massive success relies on a simple principle: it is designed not as a corporate social responsibility project or a charity case, but as a highly profitable financial investment for businesses and a prestigious career accelerator for students.  
Brookings Institution

Here is exactly how the system functions mechanically, financially, and logistically from both perspectives.

1. The Corporate Funding & Business Perspective
In many countries, companies view training young students as an expensive burden. In Switzerland, the private sector voluntarily funds 60% of the entire VPET ecosystem (amounting to billions of francs annually) because it makes clear economic sense.

The Cost-Benefit Equation
A host company pays for the apprentice’s salary, their in-house supervisor's time, and industry-specific external courses. The gross cost to a company averages between CHF 20,000 and CHF 30,000 per year per apprentice.  
Bundesinstitut für Berufsbildung | BIBB

However, extensive economic studies by the Swiss Federal University for Vocational Education and Training (SFUVET) consistently reveal a striking reality: nearly 60% of Swiss companies break even or turn a net profit by the end of the apprenticeship.  
Brookings Institution

Financial Element	Description
Gross Costs	Apprentice salary, supervisor/trainer hours, tools, materials, and specialized branch courses.
Productive Output	Real, commercial work performed by the apprentice for the company's clients.
The Bottom Line	The average productive output (CHF 18,000 to CHF 36,000/year) completely offsets or exceeds the gross training costs, yielding a net positive Return on Investment (ROI).
Real Work vs. Mock Exercises
Swiss companies achieve a positive ROI because apprentices are integrated into standard operations from day one. They do not shadow workers or do fake practice tasks.  
Brookings Institution

A 16-year-old banking apprentice handles real customer documentation and helps process real mortgage applications under supervision.  
Brookings Institution

A polymechanic apprentice programs multi-million-dollar CNC machinery to manufacture precision parts sold to clients.

Slashing Recruitment and Onboarding Costs
By training apprentices, companies cultivate their own future workforce tailored precisely to their corporate workflow. When an apprentice graduates at age 19, the company can hire them immediately as a fully functioning professional, saving thousands of francs in external headhunter fees, onboarding friction, and the risk of a bad hire. Even if the graduate leaves, the collective industry benefits from a massive pool of highly skilled, standardized talent.  
Brookings Institution

2. The Student & Apprentice Perspective
For a 15- or 16-year-old Swiss student finishing compulsory lower-secondary school, choosing an apprenticeship is a highly sought-after, mainstream path—not a fallback for struggling students.

The Hybrid Weekly Schedule
Students sign a legally binding employment contract with a host company that splits their week across multiple distinct learning environments:  
Bundesinstitut für Berufsbildung | BIBB



Salaries scale up every year as the apprentice becomes more skilled and productive:  
Bundesinstitut für Berufsbildung | BIBB

Under Article 323 of the Swiss Civil Code, this income belongs legally to the minor to manage, though parents can request a reasonable contribution toward household expenses. It is typically low enough to fall entirely under the tax-exempt threshold.  
Moneyland.ch

3. The "Permeability" Lifeline (No Dead Ends)
The most brilliant design aspect of the Swiss system from the student perspective is permeability (Durchlässigkeit). Choosing an apprenticeship at age 15 does not mean locking oneself out of higher education or high-level academic careers.

 
 Students can elect to take additional evening or weekend classes to earn a Federal Vocational Baccalaureate (FVB) alongside or right after their apprenticeship.  
About Switzerland

This qualification grants them direct, exam-free entry into Universities of Applied Sciences (Fachhochschulen) to pursue advanced degrees in engineering, business, or healthcare.  
About Switzerland
+ 1

With a supplementary bridging exam (the Passarelle), they can even pivot entirely into traditional research universities, like ETH Zurich or EPFL.  
Swissinfo

This total flexibility eliminates parental anxiety about "tracking" their children too early, ensuring that the labor market gets highly technical youth immediately, while the academic pipeline remains open for late bloomers.

If you want to look deeper into the structural mechanics of the system:

Analyze the Federal Vocational Baccalaureate

Examine government and cantonal funding splits

How are public funds split between the Swiss Confederation and individual cantons to maintain vocational schools?

The funding of the Swiss Vocational and Professional Education and Training (VPET) system is built on a highly structured tripartite model involving the Swiss Confederation (federal government), the cantons (states), and professional trade associations.

While the private sector and trade associations cover the costs of on-the-job training and industry-specific branch courses, the public sector is entirely responsible for funding, maintaining, and operating the vocational schools (Berufsfachschulen).

Within the public sector, the financial and administrative burden is strictly divided between the federal level and the cantonal level, dictated by a precise legal framework.

1. The Statutory Split: The 25% Rule
The foundational blueprint for public funding is anchored in the Swiss Federal Vocational and Professional Education and Training Act (VPETA). Under this law, a clear cap and target are set for how much the federal government must contribute:

The Federal Mandate: The Swiss Confederation is legally required to cover exactly one-quarter (25%) of the total public expenditure dedicated to the VPET system.

Consequently, the remaining 75% of public funding is covered collectively by the 26 individual cantons.

Public Level	Funding Share	Primary Financial Responsibility
Swiss Confederation (Federal)	25%	Strategic steering, quality assurance, and block grants distributed to the cantons.
Cantons (State/Local)	75%	Operational execution, teacher salaries, school infrastructure, and local administration.
2. How the Federal Funds are Distributed: Block Grants
The Confederation does not fund individual vocational schools or pay local teachers directly. Instead, the federal share is managed by the State Secretariat for Education, Research and Innovation (SERI) and distributed to the cantons primarily through performance-related block grants (Pauschalbeiträge).

To ensure fairness and account for the vastly different economic sizes of the cantons (e.g., Zurich vs. Uri), the federal government calculates these block grants using a specific formula based on two main criteria:

The number of apprentices registered in vocational schools within that specific canton.

The structural cost indicators of the training programs offered (for instance, training an advanced precision machinist or a laboratory technician requires significantly more expensive school infrastructure and machinery than training a commercial clerk).

Additionally, the Confederation reserves roughly 10% of its total budget to fund projects of national importance, such as developing new digital learning platforms, optimizing training for emerging industries, or supporting highly specialized, low-volume professions that don't have regional schools.

3. The Cantonal Burden: Infrastructure and Operations
Because the cantons contribute the lion's share (75%) of the public budget, they retain complete operational control over the vocational schools. The cantons are the actual "owners" and operators of the educational infrastructure.

Cantonal funding is funneled directly into day-to-day operations:

Teacher Salaries: The cantons hire, certify, and pay the salaries of vocational school teachers, aligning with cantonal civil service pay scales.

School Infrastructure: Building, maintaining, heating, and digitally equipping the physical vocational school buildings and school laboratories.

Career Guidance: Funding the regional vocational counseling centers that help 15-year-old students find open apprenticeship positions in the private market.

4. The Inter-Cantonal Agreement (IegV)
A major logistical challenge in a decentralized country like Switzerland is that not every canton can host a specialized vocational school for every single one of the country's 250+ distinct professions. For example, a minor canton might only have a handful of apprentice watchmakers or chimney sweeps, making it financially impossible to build a dedicated school or hire specialized instructors locally.

To solve this, the cantons operate under a legally binding framework called the Inter-Cantonal Agreement on Funding for Vocational Education and Training (IegV).

Under this agreement, if an apprentice lives and works in Canton A, but the closest or most appropriate vocational school for their specific trade is located in Canton B, the apprentice crosses borders to attend school.Canton A (the home canton) is then legally required to pay a standardized, pre-negotiated per-capita tuition fee directly to Canton B (the host canton) to compensate for the operational and infrastructure costs of educating their resident. This seamless inter-cantonal clearinghouse ensures that every apprentice in the country has access to high-quality, specialized education, regardless of how small or rural their home canton is.

It looks like your question has a couple of typos—by "the heaven of comoneisy," you mean the havens of communism.To answer this accurately, we have to look at the phrase through two different lenses. In pure Marxist theory, a "communist heaven" (a stateless, classless, moneyless society where everyone gives according to their ability and takes according to their need) has never actually existed on Earth.However, if we are talking about historical or current socialist states that achieved the highest quality of life, economic productivity, or social resilience under a communist party, a few specific countries stand out as "havens" relative to the rest of the Eastern Bloc.1. The Historical Havens (The 20th Century)During the Cold War, two nations were widely regarded as the most comfortable, prosperous, and liberal places to live under a communist government.Slovenia (as part of Socialist Yugoslavia)Yugoslavia was unique because its leader, Josip Broz Tito, broke away from Joseph Stalin's Soviet influence in 1948. Instead of the rigid, top-down Soviet "command economy," Yugoslavia implemented a system called Socialist Workers' Self-Management.How it worked: Factories and enterprises were not owned directly by the state; instead, they were owned socially, and the workers themselves voted on how to run the company, what to produce, and how to split the profits.Why it was a haven: Slovenia was the crown jewel of this system. It combined socialist welfare (free healthcare, heavily subsidized housing, and free university education) with access to Western consumer goods. Because Yugoslavia was non-aligned, citizens possessed a passport that allowed them to travel freely to both the communist East and the capitalist West—a luxury completely denied to citizens of the USSR or East Germany. Slovenia emerged from the communist era as the wealthiest, most industrialized, and most economically advanced region of the entire socialist world.East Germany (The German Democratic Republic - GDR)While politically repressive due to the heavy surveillance of the Stasi (secret police), East Germany was visually and economically the "showcase" of the Soviet sphere.Why it was a haven: The Soviet Union poured immense resources into East Germany to prove that communism could compete with West Germany. It possessed the highest productivity, the strongest infrastructure, and the most advanced technology in the Eastern Bloc.Social benefits: The GDR was incredibly advanced in terms of social safety nets, particularly for women. It provided comprehensive, completely free early-childhood daycare, guaranteed employment, and progressive maternal leave policies that allowed female labor participation to skyrocket compared to Western nations at the time.2. The Modern Havens (The 21st Century)Today, only a handful of countries are officially ruled by Communist Parties. They have survived by abandoning rigid, old-school economic planning and adopting unique hybrid models.VietnamVietnam describes its economy as a "socialist-oriented market economy." Since the Doi Moi economic reforms initiated in 1986, the Communist Party of Vietnam shifted away from collective farming toward a mixed market model, while strictly retaining political control and state ownership of core strategic industries (like energy and telecommunications).  Why it is a haven: Vietnam has become one of the global success stories of poverty reduction. In the late 1980s, it was one of the poorest countries in the world; today, it is a bustling global manufacturing hub.The balance: What makes it a modern socialist success is that the government has used this capitalist wealth generation to heavily fund social pillars. Vietnam maintains highly accessible public healthcare, remarkably high literacy rates (over 95%), and lower income inequality compared to raw, unregulated capitalist economies in the developing world.Cuba (A Haven for Social Metrics)Cuba presents a paradox. Economically, it struggles with severe shortages, crumbling infrastructure, and rationing, driven by a combination of tight state control and a decades-long US economic embargo. However, from a purely social and humanitarian standpoint, it is often viewed by left-wing theorists as a structural haven.

The Medical Superpower: Despite being a developing nation with low cash reserves, Cuba has a higher life expectancy and lower infant mortality rate than many wealthy capitalist nations (including the United States). Healthcare and education are 100% free and constitutionally guaranteed.  Exporting Aid: Cuba produces so many highly trained medical professionals that it famously exports thousands of doctors worldwide on humanitarian missions, turning its socialist education system into its primary diplomatic tool.  

Ultimately, these countries showed that while a perfect "communist utopia" remains theoretical, certain implementations of socialist policies succeeded in providing high levels of education, healthcare, and stability for their populations when compared to their peers.

If you are looking for the theological or mythological realm of eternal punishment, its location depends entirely on the religious or cultural tradition you consult. In ancient Hebrew cosmology, the dead went to Sheol, which was envisioned as a dark, silent underworld located deep beneath the surface of the Earth. Similarly, ancient Greek mythology placed the realm of Hades beneath the terrestrial world, accessible through deep caverns, volcanic fissures, or remote rivers like the Styx. In traditional Christian and Islamic theology, Hell is generally understood as a metaphysical dimension or a state of separation from God, rather than a physical coordinate you could locate with a GPS.

However, if you are looking for places named Hell that you can actually visit on a map, there are three prominent, real-world locations that bear the name.

The first is the village of Hell, located in the Stjørdal municipality of Norway. Situated in the central part of the country near the Trondheim Fjord, this small village has a population of around two thousand people. Its name has absolutely nothing to do with fire or damnation; it derives from the Old Norse word "hellir," which translates to a cliff cave or an overhanging rock shelter. In a twist of geographic irony, temperatures in this particular Hell routinely drop below minus twenty degrees Celsius during the winter, making it a popular spot for tourists who want to take photos of the local train station sign when "Hell has frozen over."  
Wikipedia

The second location is an unincorporated community called Hell, situated in the state of Michigan in the United States. Located in Livingston County, about fifteen miles northwest of Ann Arbor, this tiny town developed in the nineteenth century around a sawmill, gristmill, and tavern operated by a pioneer named George Reeves. There are several folk stories about how it got its name. One theory suggests that a pair of German travelers stepped out of a stagecoach in the 1830s and remarked, "So schön hell," meaning "so beautifully bright," which locals misinterpreted. Another popular theory is that when Reeves was asked what to name the settlement he helped build, he casually replied that he didn't care if they named it Hell. The name became official in 1841, and the town embraces its identity today by operating a themed post office and selling novelty souvenirs.  
Grokipedia

The third famous location is a geological site known as Hell, located in the district of West Bay on the island of Grand Cayman. This Hell is an eerie, half-acre field of jagged, blackened limestone and dolomite formations. Over millions of years, acidic rainwater and rock-eating algae eroded an ancient coral reef, leaving behind a scorched-looking landscape of sharp spires completely devoid of vegetation. Local folklore says that a British colonial official or hunter looked out over the desolate, jagged rocks in the early twentieth century and exclaimed that the area looked like Hell. Today, the Cayman Islands government owns part of the site, where visitors stop to buy souvenirs, meet a local performer dressed as the devil, and mail postcards stamped from the local Hell Post Office to prove they have been to Hell and back. 


Hello everyone. In this presentation, I'm going to talk about TransferBreaker. This is one of the research projects I worked on during my master's degree, and the paper is currently under review. The title of the paper is TransferBreaker: Breaking Adversarial Transferability in Fine-Tuned Speech Recognition. 1● Okay, let's start with some background on ASR and adversarial attacks. ● ASR, or Automatic Speech Recognition, is the task of transcribing speech into text. As shown in the figure, the model receives an audio sample and generates the corresponding transcription. In this example, the spoken phrase is "Hello World," and the model correctly transcribes it. ● However, like many deep learning systems, ASR models are vulnerable to adversarial attacks. As shown in the figure, an attacker can add a very small perturbation to the original audio signal. Although this perturbation is almost imperceptible to humans, it can significantly affect the model's prediction. ● For a human listener, both audio samples sound essentially the same. However, the ASR model is highly sensitive to these perturbations and may produce a completely different transcription. In this example, the original phrase "Hello World" is transformed into a meaningless sequence of characters. This is an example of an untargeted adversarial attack. ● When discussing adversarial attacks, we typically consider two settings. ● The first is the white-box setting, where the attacker has full access to the model, including its parameters and gradients. This allows the attacker to directly optimize adversarial perturbations using gradient-based methods. ● The second is the black-box setting, where the attacker has no access to the model parameters. Instead, they can only interact with the system through its inputs and outputs. For example, they can submit an audio sample and observe the resulting transcription. ● A common assumption is that deploying models as black-box systems provides security. While black-box deployment can certainly make attacks more difficult, in this work we show that it does not guarantee security. 2● Now, let me explain the motivation behind TransferBreaker. ● Today, when building an ASR system, the common approach is to start with a powerful open-source pretrained model, such as Whisper or Wav2Vec2, and fine-tune it on the target language or task. ● This approach is widely used because these models already contain rich pretrained knowledge, provide strong support for low-resource languages, and have proven architectures. ● However, this leads to an interesting security question. ● We have a public base model that is accessible to everyone, and a privately fine-tuned target model that is deployed by an organization. ● Our key observation is that adversarial attacks crafted on the public base model can successfully transfer to the privately fine-tuned target model, even though the attacker has no access to the target model itself. ● This observation motivates our work. The goal of TransferBreaker is to develop a fine-tuning framework that breaks this adversarial transferability and improves the robustness of the target model. ● As shown in the figure, fine-tuning significantly improves recognition performance under normal conditions. For example, on Polish, the Word Error Rate drops to around 9.6%. However, when adversarial examples generated on the public base model are applied to the fine-tuned model, the Word Error Rate increases dramatically to more than 86%. ● We observe the same behavior across different languages and architectures, showing that fine-tuning alone does not eliminate adversarial transferability. ● Therefore, the goal of this paper is to develop a fine-tuning framework that makes target ASR models robust against transferable adversarial attacks. 3● Now, let me briefly define our threat model and problem setting. ● We assume that the attacker has white-box access to the public base model. In other words, the attacker has access to the model parameters and gradients and can generate adversarial perturbations using the base model. ● However, the attacker has no access to the privately fine-tuned target model. ● The attacker's goal is to generate adversarial perturbations on the public base model that successfully transfer to the target model. ● We consider the attack successful if these perturbations significantly increase the Word Error Rate, or WER, and the Character Error Rate, or CER, of the target model. ● At the same time, the perturbations should preserve perceptual similarity. In other words, when a human listens to the audio, the content should sound unchanged, even though the model's transcription is severely affected. 4● Adversarial robustness has been extensively studied, particularly in image classification, where adversarial training—or adversarial fine-tuning—is the most effective defense. ● The idea is simple: during training, we generate adversarial examples that maximize the model’s loss, and then optimize the model to minimize the loss on these examples. This forms a min–max optimization problem between the attacker and the model. ● However, standard AFT faces major challenges in ASR. Unlike image classification, ASR is a sequence generation problem with a large vocabulary space, making the optimization significantly more complex. ● More importantly, standard AFT tries to suppress adversarial perturbations across the entire white-box perturbation space. This means adversarial examples are continuously generated using gradients of the current model, enforcing robustness against all possible white-box attacks. ● In our setting, however, the attacker generates adversarial examples on a public base model and transfers them to a privately fine-tuned target model. This means we mainly care about transferable perturbations, not the entire white-box space. ● This mismatch is the core limitation of standard AFT: by targeting the full perturbation space, it introduces an overly broad objective and overly strong constraints on the model, which can reduce its effectiveness against the transferred attacks that actually matter in our scenario. 5TransferBreaker Framework Overview ● Based on our threat model and the observed failure of standard baselines, we are motivated to propose TransferBreaker, a fine-tuning framework designed to break base-to-target transferability. ● TransferBreaker consists of three core components: Base-AFT, LJ-Regularization, and HybridGrad-AFT. In the following slides, we will explain each component in detail. ● 6● As discussed earlier, standard AFT is misaligned with our threat model. In our setting, the attacker generates adversarial perturbations using gradients from a public base model. ● In Base-AFT, we address this directly by augmenting the training data with adversarial examples generated from the fixed base model, rather than the continually updated target model. ● Empirically, this leads to significantly stronger robustness against transfer attacks. ● This works because it focuses on transferable directions that align with the attacker’s objective, while also reducing optimization complexity since the base model remains fixed (frozen). ● Theoretically, we show that Base-AFT reduces the target model’s sensitivity along directions where the base model is most vulnerable. 7● The second component of TransferBreaker is Latent Jacobian Regularization, or LJ-Regularization. ● Our motivation comes from an interesting observation. We found a strong correlation between adversarial Character Error Rate, or CER, and latent-space deviation caused by adversarial perturbations. ● In this figure, the x-axis shows the L2-normalized distance between the latent representations of clean and adversarial samples, while the y-axis shows the adversarial CER. As you can see, as the latent distance increases, the adversarial CER also increases. ● This observation suggests that adversarial attacks are closely related to changes in the latent representation. Therefore, we introduce LJ-Regularization to enforce latent invariance. ● The idea is to encourage similar latent representations for a clean audio sample and its adversarial counterpart. More specifically, LJ-Regularization minimizes the L2 distance between the latent representation of the adversarial sample produced by the target model and the latent representation of the corresponding clean sample produced by the frozen base model. ● From a theoretical perspective, we show that this regularization effectively regularizes the latent Jacobian along adversarially vulnerable directions. I have omitted the mathematical details here for simplicity, but they are discussed in the paper. 8● In adversarial robustness, a common concern is adaptive attacks. In other words, what happens if the attacker knows the defense strategy exactly? ● In our setting, an attacker could train a surrogate TransferBreaker model using the same Base-AFT and LJ-Regularization components. As a result, the surrogate model may become much more similar to the target model than the original public base model, giving the attacker near white-box-level access. ● This creates a challenge. Base-AFT and LJ-Regularization are effective against standard transfer attacks, but they may not be sufficient against such adaptive attacks. ● Traditionally, the strongest defense in the white-box setting is Standard Adversarial Fine-Tuning, or Standard-AFT. However, as we discussed earlier, Standard-AFT optimizes over the entire perturbation space, leading to an overly broad objective and excessive constraints on the model. ● To address this limitation, we introduced Base-AFT. However, in the adaptive attack setting, Base-AFT alone is no longer sufficient. ● This motivates the third component of TransferBreaker: HybridGrad-AFT. 9TransferBreaker: HybridGrad-AFT ● We propose HybridGrad, a mechanism that combines gradients from both the base model and the target model to generate perturbations that are effective on both models. ● The key idea behind HybridGrad-AFT is to improve robustness in the fundamental transferable directions. By focusing on perturbations that affect both models, we restrict adversarial training to the transferable subspace and avoid full perturbation-space optimization. ● As a result, HybridGrad-AFT provides targeted robustness against transferable attacks while avoiding the excessive constraints introduced by standard adversarial fine-tuning. ● 10Method: TransferBlock Framework Now, we can see all the components of TransferBreaker together in a single framework. First, we generate the Base-AFT perturbation using the public base model. We also generate a HybridGrad perturbation by combining gradients from both the base model and the target model. During training, we use a Bernoulli variable to randomly select either the Base-AFT perturbation or the HybridGrad perturbation. The selected perturbation is then applied to the input audio. Next, we feed the clean audio sample into both the frozen base model and the target model. We train the target model using the original ASR loss on both the clean and perturbed audio samples. In addition, we apply LJ-Regularization to encourage similar latent representations for clean and adversarial samples, improving latent-space invariance and robustness. Together, these three components—Base-AFT, HybridGrad-AFT, and LJ-Regularization—form the complete TransferBreaker framework. 11Here, you can see the main results table from the paper. We evaluate TransferBreaker across three languages and four different ASR models. For simplicity, I will focus on this block, which corresponds to the Polish language and the Whisper-Medium model. In the first column, we have the public base model. Under normal conditions, it achieves a WER of about 15%. However, under adversarial attack, the WER increases dramatically to 118%. Next, we fine-tune the model on the Common Voice dataset. This improves the clean WER to around 9%, showing that fine-tuning is effective for the ASR task itself. However, the WER under transferred adversarial attacks is still about 86%, indicating that the fine-tuned model remains highly vulnerable. In the third column, we apply Standard-AFT. This reduces the adversarial WER to around 44%, which is a significant improvement. Finally, our TransferBreaker framework further reduces the adversarial WER to approximately 24%, substantially outperforming Standard-AFT while maintaining strong performance on clean samples. Overall, these results show that TransferBreaker is highly effective at reducing adversarial transferability. 12Results: Adaptive Attacks and HybridGrad-AFT Ablation This table presents the adaptive attack ablation results for the same language and model. The results show that Base-AFT alone is not sufficient in this setting. Under adaptive attacks, Base-AFT achieves an adversarial WER of about 57%. Combining Base-AFT with Standard-AFT improves robustness and reduces the adversarial WER to around 46%. However, our full TransferBreaker framework achieves the best result, reducing the adversarial WER further to approximately 44%. These results demonstrate that HybridGrad-AFT provides additional robustness against adaptive attacks beyond what can be achieved with Base-AFT alone. 13Results: Base-AFT Ablation We also perform an ablation study on Base-AFT and Std-AFT, where you can see that Base-AFT consistently performs better. 14Results: LJ-Regularization Ablation We also conduct an ablation study on the presence and absence of LJ-regularization, which shows that LJ-regularization consistently improves performance under transfer attacks 15Conclusion ● TransferBreaker Components: A Unified Fine-Tuning Framework for Breaking Transferability of Adversarial Perturbations ○ Base-AFT ○ HybridGrad-AFT ○ Latent Jacobian Regularization ● Key Contributions ○ Exposes a critical transferability vulnerability in fine-tuned ASR systems ○ Introduces Base-AFT for attacker-aligned adversarial training ○ Develops Latent Jacobian Regularization for latent invariance ○ Proposes HybridGrad-AFT for adaptive robustness ○ Demonstrates effectiveness across multiple languages and architectures 16Thank You for Your Attention!
